<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="1e3d884044a946d795a872e0ab3de7e1"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1e3d884044a946d795a872e0ab3de7e1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2caea69b-fc20-458c-87f4-eaf5d27a435a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d22889257" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) using solid-state polymer electrolytes (SPEs) show impressive superiority in energy density and safety. As promising candidates for SPEs, solid-state plastic crystal electrolytes (SPCE) based on succinonitrile (SN) plastic crystal could achieve high ion conductivity and wide voltage window. Nonetheless, the notorious SN decomposition reaction on the electrode/electrolyte interface seriously challenges the stable operation of the battery. To address this drawback, we commence with the structural engineering of the polymer chain segments in SPCE and employ intermolecular interactions to optimize the composition of solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Moreover, this study emphasizes the importance of polymer network design in optimizing the migration behavior of sodium ions in SPCE. The assembled sodium symmetric cells display a high critical current density of up to 2.7 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and stable cycling performance for 700 hours at 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Furthermore, the Na/SPCE-9/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> maintains a discharge specific capacity of up to 76.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C and shows impressive long-cycle stability, retaining 86.2 % of initial capacity over 5000 cycles with an average coulombic efficiency of 99.9 %. Our work presents a high-performance SPCE with intrinsic safety, providing valuable insights for the future design of solid-state SMBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202418959-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202418959-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent decades, the cost-effective, widely available sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) have prompted their consideration as potential alternatives to lithium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0001_R_d22889249e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#ange202418959-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0004_R_d22889249e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> Sodium metal, with its low redox potential (−2.71 V vs. the standard hydrogen electrode) and high theoretical capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), is an ideal anode material for SMBs.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0005_R_d22889249e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Energy">5</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0006_R_d22889249e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">6</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, using conventional liquid electrolytes (LE) results in the uncontrolled growth of sodium dendrites at the anode side, causing short circuit in SMBs.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0007_R_d22889249e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a>-<a href="#ange202418959-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0009_R_d22889249e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span> Solid electrolytes (SE) appear to be the ultimate solution to address the above issues.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0010_R_d22889249e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#ange202418959-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0012_R_d22889249e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs), as a viable option for SE, demonstrate impressive processability and adaptability in harsh external environments.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0013_R_d22889249e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span> However, the ionic conductivity of conventional SPEs is significantly lower than that of LE and inorganic SE, which hinders their direct application in SMBs at room temperature (RT). Introducing plasticizers to reduce polymer crystallinity and facilitate salt solvation effects is considered an effective solution to this issue.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0014_R_d22889249e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> Commonly used plasticizers are organic solvents including ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), and diethyl carbonate (DEC).<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0015_R_d22889249e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span> These solvents enhance the amorphous region of the polymer and salt dissociation, thereby improving ionic conductivity. However, the use of SPEs containing organic solvent plasticizers does not completely eliminate safety risks.</p>
         
         <p>Succinonitrile (SN) is an intrinsically non-flammable plasticizer that can enhance the ion conductivity of SPEs by improving segmental motion.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0016_R_d22889249e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0017_R_d22889249e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> In addition, the large dipole of the C≡N bond in SN enables it to dissolve a diverse range of salts, thereby forming deep eutectic electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0018_R_d22889249e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> Benefiting from these characteristics, solid-state plastic crystal electrolyte (SPCE) incorporating SN has been extensively investigated in alkali metal battery system.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0019_R_d22889249e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature">19</a>-<a href="#ange202418959-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0021_R_d22889249e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span> However, the detrimental side reaction of SN precludes its use as the primary electrolyte component. Due to its poor reduction stability, SN tends to polymerize under the catalysis of Li/Na metals, leading to irreversible anode corrosion.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0022_R_d22889249e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">22</a></sup></span> Additionally, the α-hydrogen atoms in SN are easily extracted, resulting in the simultaneous formation of hydrogen gas.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0023_R_d22889249e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span> Targeting the above issues, efforts have been made such as the selection of an electron-rich additive to anchor the α-H of SN and modulate the solvation structure,<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0024_R_d22889249e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> or the pretreatment of Na metal to construct a NaF-rich interlayer.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0025_R_d22889249e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Sci.">25</a></sup></span> In addition, the effect of Fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive on the SN-based polymer electrolytes was investigated in detail, revealing that FEC can be preferentially reduced to NaF and form dome-type Na<sup>0</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0026_R_d22889249e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span> However, to the best of our knowledge, optimizing ion transport in SPCE through the rational design of polymer network structures is rarely reported. Meanwhile, the impact of dipole-dipole interactions between the polymer backbone and SN on solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation remains unclear.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we employed a specific proportion of ester-rich polymer monomers to in situ solidify SN-based deep eutectic electrolytes (DEE), thereby preparing a novel SPCE-9 for SMBs. In SPCE-9, a “biphase coexistence” Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathway is constructed. Concretely, Na<sup>+</sup> is mainly dependent on the solvated sheath [SN⋅⋅⋅Na<sup>+</sup>] to enable their migration within the SN phase efficiently. Meanwhile, the introduction of the polymer backbone enhances the dissociation of NaTFSI. The polar sites on the segments synergize with SN to provide additional transport pathways for Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition, robust van der Waals interactions between the polymer backbone and the free SN in SPCE immobilize the free SN and reduce the nitrogen content in SEI generated from SN decomposition. As a result, the prepared Na/SPCE-9/NVP battery can withstand 5000 cycles at 2 C without any short-circuit phenomenon, maintaining an average coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.9 %, which is much superior to Na/DEE/NVP battery. These results validate the significance of designing polymer network to modulate the ion transfer behaviors and SEI components. Our work proposed valuable prospective insights for future solid-state SMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202418959-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202418959-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ange202418959-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ange202418959-sec-0003-title"> Design Principle and Preparation Process</h3>
            
            <p>Rational design of polymer network could modulate the ion transport mechanism in solid plastic crystal electrolytes (SPCE). Utilizing a single linear polymer monomer may lead to phase separation in electrolyte bulk, which has been extensively investigated in Lithium battery systems.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0019_R_d22889249e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature">19</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0020_R_d22889249e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0027_R_d22889249e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span> The high ion conductivity in this electrolyte is attributed to the fast migration pathway in the plastic crystal phase, but the aggregate polymer phase limits ion migration. High-functionality monomers can avoid phase separation in SN-based electrolytes. However, excessive polymer cross-linking will break the continuous plastic crystal phase, which is unfavorable for efficient ion transport (Figure S1a). To address this, Ethoxylated trimethylolpropane triacrylate (ETPTA) and trimethylene glycol dimethacrylate (TEGDMA) were copolymerized to prepare SPCE-9 in this work. The ETPTA provides sufficient cross-linked sites while the TEGDMA offers a continuous migration pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S1b). Several control experiments were conducted to support this conclusion: EPCE (10 % ETPTA to solidify DEE), TPCE (10 % TEGDMA to solidify DEE), and PPCE (same polymer ratio as SPCE-9, but with Pentaerythritol tetraacrylate (PETEA) replacing TEGDMA for a highly crosslinked matrix in the plastic-crystal electrolyte). As shown in Figure S1c, SPCE-9 delivers the lowest impedance, verifying the rationale of this polymer matrix design.</p>
            
            <p>The synthesized SPCE consists of two parts: an ester-rich polymer network as a backbone, and the DEE which contains SN and NaTFSI (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0001">1a</a>). FEC was added in DEE as an additive to prevent side reaction during polymerization. SPCE-6, SPCE-7, SPCE-8, and SPCE-9 correspond to the polymer volume ratio of 40 %, 30 %, 20 %, and 10 % respectively. To decide the optimal polymer network ratio, the mechanical performance and polymerization states were compared, as shown in Figure S2. Considering the balance between ionic conductivity and mechanical strength, SPCE-9 was selected as the optimal ratio. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure S3, the Electrostatic Potential (ESP) and Electron Localization Function (ELF) analysis were conducted to analyze the electron distribution of the main components in SPCE-9. The electron-withdrawing nitrile group reduces the electron density of the α-H atoms in SN significantly. Herein, the polymer segments provide strong intermolecular interaction to anchor free SN by the electron-riched C=O groups. After combining with polymer segments, the ESP of the α-H atoms in SN decreased, demonstrating an efficient anchoring effect that enhanced SN stability, which is favorable for reducing the SN side reactions on the Na metal.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ange202418959-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b249d817-259f-4fc5-8714-5abf817a2d38/ange202418959-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b249d817-259f-4fc5-8714-5abf817a2d38/ange202418959-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d62415f-b4b0-4406-bfb4-cd4175ee05ad/ange202418959-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b249d817-259f-4fc5-8714-5abf817a2d38/ange202418959-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202418959-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202418959" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Design principle and morphology illustration of SPCE: a) The molecule interaction and Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway schematic picture of SPCE. b) Electrostatic potentials of ETPTA, SN, TEGDMA, ETPTA-SN, and TEGDMA-SN. c) TG-FTIR 3D spectrum image of SPCE-9. d) XRM-CT 3D reconstruction image of Glass Fiber separator and SPCE-9. e) XRM-CT Z axis slice picture of SPCE-9 in 100 μm and 200 μm.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The SPCE precursor with good fluidity was directly combined with GF/F supporting separator during battery assembly. It underwent complete in situ polymerization after heat treatment at 70 °C for 3 hours, resulting in a stable, non-fluid SPCE. The structural stability of SPCE-9 was evidenced by its stable physical morphology and absence of phase separation even after 96 hours of storage. This conclusion was also supported by Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) results (Figure S4). The polymerization reaction of the monomer solution was characterized by FT-IR spectra of SPCE-9 (Figure S5). The characteristic peak of C=C bonds from TEGDMA and ETPTA becomes indistinct in SPCE, indicating complete polymerization of the monomer solution. There is no noticeable change in the peak at 1715 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 2253 cm<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating C=O in polymer segments and C≡N in SN remains unaffected by the polymerization process. The Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR) <sup>13</sup>C spectrum further demonstrated this process. Figure S6 shows the discernible <sup>13</sup>C characteristic peaks of SN and FEC in the SPCE-9 spectra. The absence of the C=C characteristic peaks from the monomers before polymerization confirms successful polymerization completion. Additionally, the peaks corresponding to various chemical environments of carbon on the polymer backbone have been identified and labeled. Notably, the carbonyl carbon signals on the polymerized backbones are obscured in the SPCE-9 NMR spectra. This is due to two factors: firstly, the carbonyl carbon signal is narrow, making it more susceptible to the electronic environment. Secondly, the carbonyl carbon signal is affected by stronger interactions with SN and sodium ions that occur after recombination with DEE, increasing the shielding effect on carbonyl carbon. Scanning electron microscope (SEM) images compared the microstructure of GF/F, GF/D, and cellulose membrane (CM), as well as the surface morphology of SPCE-9 (Figure S7). Their ion conductivity and membrane thickness have been measured. CM shows a limited electrolyte filling, resulting in the lowest ionic conductivity. GF/D, with its loose structure, shows the highest ionic conductivity, but its poor wettability requires addition of excess precursor (&gt;240 μl) to ensure adequate wetting. The SPCE-9 formed on GF/D was much thicker than on GF/F and CM. Based on these results, we conclude that GF/F is the most suitable membrane for this work. X-ray microscopy computed tomography (XRM-CT) was used to detect internal structures and further validate the physical properties of SPCE.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0028_R_d22889249e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0001">1d</a>-e shows a large number of voids inside the GF/F while the voids are filled evenly in SPCE. There is no visible crack in it. In the ignition test illustrated in Figure S8, the conventional LE caught fire easily, whereas the prepared SPCE and DEE did not ignite, demonstrating the intrinsic safety of DEE and SPCE. To further demonstrate the superiority of safety, the TG-FTIR test was conducted, as shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure S9. During pyrolysis, SPCE-9 generates CO<sub>2</sub>, NH<sub>3</sub>, as well as H<sub>2</sub>O, reducing the concentration of flammable gases and the risks of safety.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ange202418959-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ange202418959-sec-0004-title"> Migration Mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup></h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the effect of polymer backbones on regulating ion transport behaviors, elaborate analyses were conducted. Solvation structure and coordination behaviors were obtained through molecular dynamic (MD) simulations, revealing the corresponding radial distribution function (RDF) and coordination number (CN). Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2a</a> shows a pure SPE model (without the addition of SN) used to verify the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer behaviors without SN. As exhibited in the radial distribution function (RDF) of Na−O (TFSI<sup>−</sup>) and Na−O (Polymer network) (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2b</a>), the presence of sharp peaks at 0.206 nm indicates an insufficient dissociation of NaTFSI. CN values of Na−O (TFSI<sup>−</sup>) and Na−O (Polymer matrix) are 1.3 and 3.7. We believe that the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> depends merely on the O atoms on the polymer chain segments in the SPE model. Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2e</a> shows a new sharp peak attributed to Na<sup>+</sup>-N (SN) at 0.206 nm for the SPCE-9 model (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2d</a>), indicating that partial TFSI<sup>−</sup> are replaced by SN. The representative solvation structures in SPCE-9 extracted from MD results are displayed in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2c</a>. The solvation energy of these structures is much higher than the binding energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and O atoms of the polymer segment (Figure S10), demonstrating the high dissociation of NaTFSI with the assistance of SN. Moreover, the RDF and CN of Na−O (Polymer) in both SPE and SPCE-9 are similar, indicating that the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> across polymer chain segments and in solvation sheath occur simultaneously. The schematic diagram of the Na<sup>+</sup> transport mechanism in SPCE is displayed in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2f</a>. This mechanism is defined as the “biphase coexistence” mechanism. In contrast to the previously reported transport mechanisms observed in SN-based plastic-crystal electrolytes,<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0019_R_d22889249e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nature">19</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0027_R_d22889249e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0029_R_d22889249e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0030_R_d22889249e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Sci.">30</a></sup></span> this study presents an effective approach for constructing ion transport channels through the strategic integration of the polymer backbone and the plastic-crystal phase. This is achieved through meticulous design of the polymer backbone structure. To deeply verify this mechanism, Raman, FT-IR, and <sup>23</sup>Na solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (ssNMR) were utilized to elucidate the dissociation of NaTFSI and the mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>. Raman spectrum (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2g</a>) shows that the peak at 746.7 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the TFSI<sup>−</sup> bonded with Na<sup>+</sup> in NaTFSI. However, in SPCE-9 and DEE, the adsorption band of TFSI<sup>−</sup> decreases to 743.1 and 741.3 cm<sup>−1</sup> respectively. This indicates that the chemical environment of TFSI<sup>−</sup> has changed as the SN and polymer matrix occupy the inner solvation sheaths of Na<sup>+</sup>. Figure S11–12 show the role of polymer network in modulating solvated SN. In DEE, the proportion of solvated SN as a percentage of the total SN is 14.36 %, whereas in SPCE-9, this value is 10.23 %. As the polymer content increases, the ratio of solvated SN decreases because the polar groups in the polymer occupy part of the solvated structure. These results substantiate the “biphase coexistence” mechanism. Additionally, a comprehensive examination of the TFSI<sup>−</sup> Raman data validates that polymer backbones facilitate the NaTFSI dissociation. After introducing the polymer backbone, the proportion of TFSI<sup>−</sup> in the ion aggregate state was significantly reduced, further supporting the enhanced dissociation effect of the “biphase coexistence” mechanism (Figure S13). As listed in Figure S14, the C≡N peak at 2252 cm<sup>−1</sup> in SN slightly rises to 2254 cm<sup>−1</sup> in DEE and SPCE-9, suggesting the formation of solvation shell structure [SN⋅⋅⋅Na<sup>+</sup>]. Meanwhile, the vibration peak of CH<sub>2</sub> at 1425 cm<sup>−1</sup> in SN rises to 1427 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1429 cm<sup>−1</sup> in DEE and SPCE-9 respectively, which further substantiates the chemical environment change in SN. In addition, varied degrees of peak shifting at 1197 cm<sup>−1</sup> (CF<sub>3</sub> in TFSI<sup>−</sup>), 1321 cm<sup>−</sup> (O=S=O in TFSI<sup>−</sup>), and 1054 cm<sup>−1</sup> (S−N−S of TFSI<sup>−</sup>) in NaTFSI spectra convincingly illustrate the sufficient dissociation of NaTFSI in SPCE-9 and DEE. The <sup>23</sup>Na Solid-State Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (ssNMR) was conducted to further illustrate the Na<sup>+</sup> migration mechanism (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0002">2h</a>). The signal at −13.2 ppm is attributed to the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR resonance of NaTFSI, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> in salts is bounded with TFSI<sup>−</sup>. A broad <sup>23</sup>Na peak of SPE is situated at −11.4 ppm, which signifies the amalgamation of three distinct chemical environments of Na<sup>+</sup>, namely NaTFSI, contact ion pairs, and Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated with ester groups on the polymer backbone. Besides, the <sup>23</sup>Na signal shifts to −8.5 ppm at SPCE-9. The increasing <sup>23</sup>Na resonance demonstrates an improved dissociation of NaTFSI attributing to SN, which is beneficial for more efficient migration of Na<sup>+</sup> in SPCE-9.
</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ange202418959-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ange202418959-sec-0005-title"> Molecular Locking Effect and SEI Formation Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Utilizing the molecular orbital theory to uncover the potential mechanism of introducing a polymer backbone on SEI formation. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">3a</a>, the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy of SN is −0.75 eV, which is the lowest among all components, indicating high susceptibility to reduction on the anode side. The LUMO energy of SN increases to −0.46 eV when combined with the polymer segment ETPTA and to −0.55 eV with TEGDMA, indicating enhanced stability towards the sodium metal anode. In-depth X-ray photo-electron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was performed to assess the surface component of cycled and pristine sodium metal anode in SPCE-9 and DEE electrolytes. The cycled SEI samples were obtained from sodium symmetric batteries subjected to a charge/discharge cycle at a capacity density of 0.1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for 50 cycles. The pristine samples were obtained from sodium symmetric batteries stored for the same period. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">3b</a>–<a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">c</a> and Figure S15, the primary inorganic components of SEI in SPCE-9 batteries are NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Figure S16 illustrates that the SEI surface primarily comprises C and O elements initially, while F and Na element ratio grows significantly as the etch level increases. NaF is a crucial component of SEI in SMBs to protect Na<sup>0</sup> from continuous electrolyte depletion, especially in SN-based electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0025_R_d22889249e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Sci.">25</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0026_R_d22889249e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span> It is widely accepted that the coupling of NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> can harmonize the mechanical strength and ion transport capacity of the SEI.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0031_R_d22889249e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem">31</a></sup></span> The Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> component in the SEI inner layer is typically derived from the two-electron reduction reaction of ester species when the passivation layer is thin.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0032_R_d22889249e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Synth. Met.">32</a></sup></span> Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in SEI is observed in SPCE-9 at both 60 and 120 s of etching, while only a minor amount appears in DEE at 120 s (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure S17), as polymer backbone in SPCE-9 is involved in SEI film formation. As demonstrated in Figure S16 and Table S2, the atomic percentage of the N element is only 2.51 % in the SEI surface layer of SPCE-9, while the percentage for DEE is 5.22 %, which confirms that the polymer network has sharply attenuated the SN decomposition on the anode side. Figure S18 shows no discernible difference N 1s spectrum of SEI formed in SPCE-9 before and after cycling, indicating that SEI formation effectively attenuates SN side effects. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM) displays an organic–inorganic hybrid SEI structure in SPCE-9 (Figure S19). The main inorganic components are NaF (PDF#36–1455), NaN<sub>3</sub> (PDF#01–0928), and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (PDF#37–0451), which is consistent with the results of XPS. To further substantiate SEI component distribution, Time-of-Flight Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry (ToF-SIMS) enables the detection of second ion fragments generated under the impact of Bi<sup>+</sup>. Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure S20 show that NaF<sup>−</sup> and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> ions in SEI originate from NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, with their content rising significantly with increasing sputtering depth, thereby verifying the distribution of inorganic components in SEI. The CHO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> fragment represents the organic components in SEI, showing a declining trend with increasing sputtering depth. The presence of inorganic NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in the inner layer, confirmed by ToF-SIMS, in-depth XPS analysis, and HR-TEM, inhibits SN side reactions and ensures the long-term stability of the cell, enhancing the overall performance. To further visualize the intermolecular interaction forces between SN and the polymer network, Interaction Region Indicator (IRI) images were presented in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0003">3e</a>.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0033_R_d22889249e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chemistry Methods">33</a></sup></span> In the IRI images, blue areas represent covalent bonds, while green areas show van der Waals interactions between the α-H in SN and C=O groups in ETPTA and TEGDMA monomers. Attributing to the interaction, the molecular polarity index (MPI) of SN molecular was reduced obviously (Table S1), which is obtained from Multiwfn software.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0034_R_d22889249e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Comput. Chem.">34</a></sup></span> The MPI of SN decreases from 0.91 to 0.59 with ETPTA and to 0.50 with TEGDMA, indicating enhanced stability of SN with the polymer network.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ange202418959-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20e9584b-8e1b-4a87-9256-e000abe08bd6/ange202418959-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20e9584b-8e1b-4a87-9256-e000abe08bd6/ange202418959-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e52b381-3250-4f8e-b34b-345c22547a88/ange202418959-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20e9584b-8e1b-4a87-9256-e000abe08bd6/ange202418959-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202418959-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202418959" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ange202418959-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ange202418959-sec-0006-title"> Electrochemical Tests and Na Metal Interface Stability</h3>
            
            <p>SPCE-6, SPCE-7, SPCE-8, and SPCE-9 represent SPCE with 60 %, 70 %, 80 %, and 90 % volume proportions of DEE. Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4a</a> illustrates an increasing trend of ionic conductivity of SPCE with increasing DEE content. Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted at various temperatures (30—80 °C) to fit the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of DEE, SPCE-9, SPCE-8, SPCE-7, and SPCE-6. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4b</a>, the relationship between ionic conductivity and temperature is consistent with the linear Arrhenius law rather than the Vogel-Fulcher-Tammann equation. It is demonstrated that the SN region primarily contributes to ion transport, which achieves high ionic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0035_R_d22889249e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span> The <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> fitting results for DEE, SPCE-9, SPCE-8, SPCE-7, and SPCE-6 are 0.220, 0.214, 0.221, 0.231, and 0.274 eV, respectively (Figure S21). These results demonstrate that as the polymer network proportion increases, the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> within the electrolyte bulk requires overcoming a higher energy barrier, resulting in sluggish kinetics. Raman mapping results of SPCE-6 and SPCE-9 validate the conclusion above (Figure S22). Surprisingly, the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> fitting results of DEE and SPCE-9 showed negligible difference. We speculate that the plasticizer effects of SN increase the mobility of polymer segments, which is favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> migration within the polymer network. To reveal the potential mechanism behind this phenomenon, the Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated structures within the polymer network were extracted and the solvation energy was calculated (Figure S23). The Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated structures within the polymer exhibit lower solvation energies. It is the efficient desolvation process that balances the ions migration energy barrier across the phase boundaries. The high ionic conductivity and relatively low Na<sup>+</sup> transfer number (Figure S24) further validate that the Na<sup>+</sup> migration is mainly dependent on the solvated sheath [SN⋅⋅⋅Na<sup>+</sup>] in the SN phase.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0029_R_d22889249e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0036_R_d22889249e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Mater.">36</a></sup></span> The critical current density (CCD) reaches up to 2.7 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> in Na/SPCE-9/Na symmetric cell (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4c</a>), demonstrating exceptional stability under high current density. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4d</a>, the average CE of SPCE-9 is 93.3 %, compared to 86.1 % for DEE.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0037_R_d22889249e1009" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span> This illustrates that the employment of a polymer backbone significantly reduces the undesired depletion of sodium metal with the electrolyte. From the linear sweep voltammetry curve of Na/SPCE-9/SS and Na/DEE/SS (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4e</a>), the potential window of DEE is 4.7 V. The brilliant electrochemical stability window of DEE is attributed to the high oxidation resistance of the nitrile group in SN.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0038_R_d22889249e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span> Although the addition of polymer network decreases the potential window of SPCE-9 to 4.6 V, it remains compatible with most cathode materials utilized in sodium-ion batteries. Besides, sodium symmetric batteries were used to test the Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping behavior. At low current density (0.1–0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), the Na/SPCE-9/Na cell can consistently operate for up to 1200 hours (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4f</a>). Voltage polarization of the Na/SPCE-9/Na battery (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0004">4g</a><b>)</b> shows that the battery is capable of maintaining a steady cycle for more than 700 hours at the 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> current density and 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> capacity density. During the Na symmetric battery tests, no soft-short circuit phenomenon was observed, demonstrating that a stable electrode/electrolyte interface has been constructed. In a distinct contrast, the polarization voltage of the Na/DEE/Na cell is unstable with severe fluctuation. A short circuit of this cell occurs at 451 hours.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ange202418959-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6de7b5e7-b96d-4c1f-ac51-14c65e8d4c5e/ange202418959-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6de7b5e7-b96d-4c1f-ac51-14c65e8d4c5e/ange202418959-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ef89a13f-7d85-442f-96fc-685431a5f162/ange202418959-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6de7b5e7-b96d-4c1f-ac51-14c65e8d4c5e/ange202418959-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202418959-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202418959" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ange202418959-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ange202418959-sec-0007-title"> Electrochemical Performance of Na/SPCE-9/NVP</h3>
            
            <p>In situ EIS was used to indicate the SEI character in charge and discharge process. Using the Distribution of Relaxation Times (DRT) method could accurately analyze the EIS results.<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0039_R_d22889249e1075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Electrochimica Acta">39</a></sup></span> As shown in Figures <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5b</a>, at different States of Charge (SOC), the Na/SPCE-9/NVP exhibits smaller interface impedance than Na/DEE/NVP, confirming the role of intermolecular interactions in optimizing the SEI. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were used to evaluate the rate and long-cycle performance of solid-state Na/SPCE-9/NVP cells. For comparison purposes, liquid-state Na/DEE/NVP, solid-state Na/SPCE-8/NVP Na/SPCE-7/NVP, and Na/SPCE-6/NVP batteries were fabricated under the same condition. Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5c</a> shows that Na/SPCE-9/NVP delivers an average specific capacity of 94.5, 91.6, 90.5, and 86.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C (1 C=100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) respectively, indicating good performance at low and medium rate. Notably, at a high rate of 10 C, the capacity maintains at 76.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than that of SPCE-8, SPCE-7, and SPCE-6. Moreover, as the rate goes back to 0.5 C, the specific capacity rises back to 93.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the high reversibility of the cell. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5d</a>, the polarization voltage of the battery is approximately 0.11, 0.13, 0.18, 0.34, and 0.56 V at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C respectively.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ange202418959-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1bb34310-6a67-4fe7-b61b-d9bc451fb22b/ange202418959-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1bb34310-6a67-4fe7-b61b-d9bc451fb22b/ange202418959-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d9eed2b-c368-4756-895a-72f77fcd3c0e/ange202418959-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1bb34310-6a67-4fe7-b61b-d9bc451fb22b/ange202418959-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202418959-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202418959" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance test of Na/SPCE-9/NVP full cells. a) DRT results of in situ EIS tests of Na/DEE/NVP b) DRT results of in situ EIS tests of Na/SPCE-9/NVP c) Rate performance tests of Na/SPCE-9/NVP, Na/SPCE-8/NVP, Na/SPCE-7/NVP, and Na/SPCE-6/NVP. d) Charge/Discharge profile of Na/SPCE-9/NVP at different rates. e) Charge/discharge profile of Na/SPCE-9/NVP at 2 C for 1–2000 cycles. f) SPCE-9 long cycle performance compared to recently reported polymer electrolytes containing plasticizers. g) Cycling performance and CE of Na/SPCE-9/NVP and Na/DEE/NVP.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Besides, Na/SPCE-9/NVP shows an unprecedented long-term cycling capacity retention ratio (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5e</a>-g). The cycling performance curve (Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5g</a>) shows that the capacity of Na/DEE/NVP decreases rapidly as the cycle progresses. After approximately 1500 cycles, CE fluctuates severely, and capacity retention drops abruptly to around 50 %. In clear contrast, Na/SPCE-9/NVP demonstrates a consistent and prolonged cycling performance, retaining 86.2 % of its initial capacity and over 99.9 % CE after 5000 cycles, presenting unrivaled cycle stability. The polarization voltage of Na/DEE/NVP also increases significantly from 0.33 V (1st cycle) to 0.89 V (1600th cycle) (Figure S25), showing inferior stability compared to the 0.16 V increase of Na/SPCE-9/NVP during the first 2000 charge/discharge cycles. This phenomenon indicates that the liquid DEE undergoes serious depletion and side reactions during cycling, which further affects SEI stability and elevates the internal resistance of the battery. To demonstrate the superiority of the in situ preparation process in this work, an ex situ cell was fabricated for comparison. In Figure S26, the in situ assembled Na/SPCE-9/NVP exhibited 97.1 % capacity retention at 0.5 C for 1000 cycles with merely 0.12 V voltage polarization. Conversely, the ex situ cell exhibited poor electrochemical performance due to the incompatible contact between the electrode and electrolyte. Figure <a href="#ange202418959-fig-0005">5f</a>, Figure S27, and Table S3 compare the electrochemical performance of SPCE-9 with other SPEs containing plasticizers recently reported in SMBs, emphasizing the exceptional cycling performance of our work as expected. The Na/SPCE-9/NVP batteries presented outstanding performance among solid-state SMBs with plasticizers regarding cycling performance,<span><sup><a href="#ange202418959-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0012_R_d22889249e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0026_R_d22889249e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a>, <a href="#ange202418959-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0040_R_d22889249e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#ange202418959-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202418959-bib-0045_R_d22889249e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span> due to the efficient Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway and the locking effect between the polymer network and free SN.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202418959-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202418959-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have constructed an effective SPCE-9 through in situ polymerization of ester-rich polymer networks to solidify SN-based DEE for SMBs. The dipole-dipole interaction between polymer segments and SN decreases the polarity of SN and improves the stability of SEI. The polymer network prevents the SN side reaction on the sodium metal anode, resulting in an ultra-long cycle life for SMBs. Moreover, Na<sup>+</sup> transmits along the polymer segments and migrates in the SN phase relying on a solvation sheath structure of [SN⋅⋅⋅Na<sup>+</sup>] in SPCE. This efficient Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway was achieved through rationale design of polymer network structures, which ensures the continuous operation and high-rate performance of SMBs. It was defined as a “biphase coexistence” Na<sup>+</sup> transfer mechanism. The assembled Na/SPCE-9/NVP cells achieve excellent capacity retention of 86.2 % over 5000 cycles, corresponding to a low capacity decay of 0.0028 % per cycle, and a high specific capacity of 76.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C rate. This outcome demonstrates the exceptional electrochemical performance of SPCE-reinforced batteries, revealing a promising design approach for high-safety and high-performance SMBs in practical energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202418959-sec-0009-title"> Acknowledgments</h2>
